        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.6b15887"><meta property="og:title" content="Enhancing Sodium-Ion Storage Behaviors in TiNb2O7 by Mechanical Ball Milling"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/acsami.6b15887.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have shown extensive prospects as alternative rechargeable batteries in large-scale energy storage systems, because of the abundance and low cost of sodium. The development of high-performance cathode and anode materials is a big challenge for SIBs. As is well known, TiNb2O7 (TNO) exhibits a high capacity of ∼250 mAh g–1 with excellent capacity retention as a Li-insertion anode for lithium-ion batteries, but it has rarely been discussed as an anode for SIBs. Here, we demonstrate ball-milled TiNb2O7 (BM-TNO) as an SIB anode, which provides an average voltage of ∼0.6 V and a reversible capacity of ∼180 mAh g–1 at a current density of 15 mA g–1, and presents excellent cyclability with 95% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 500 mA g–1. A possible Na storage mechanism in BM-TNO is also proposed."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/acsami.6b15887.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da8ce1bf0bb325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8ce1bf0bb325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'e27ec31e-2d34-48ac-8fac-ad6aeb320e0a'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8ce1bf0bb325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Enhancing Sodium-Ion Storage Behaviors in TiNb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; by Mechanical Ball Milling", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b15887", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Enhancing Sodium-Ion Storage Behaviors in TiNb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; by Mechanical Ball Milling", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b15887", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Enhancing Sodium-Ion Storage Behaviors in TiNb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; by Mechanical Ball Milling", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b15887", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Enhancing Sodium-Ion Storage Behaviors in TiNb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; by Mechanical Ball Milling", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b15887", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Enhancing Sodium-Ion Storage Behaviors in TiNb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; by Mechanical Ball Milling", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b15887", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Enhancing Sodium-Ion Storage Behaviors in TiNb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; by Mechanical Ball Milling", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b15887", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Enhancing Sodium-Ion Storage Behaviors in TiNb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; by Mechanical Ball Milling", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b15887", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Enhancing Sodium-Ion Storage Behaviors in TiNb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; by Mechanical Ball Milling", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b15887", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Enhancing Sodium-Ion Storage Behaviors in TiNb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; by Mechanical Ball Milling", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b15887", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Han).</div><div class="corresp-info"><strong>*</strong>E-mail: <a href="mailto:huangyh@hust.edu.cn">huangyh@hust.edu.cn</a> (Y. Huang).</div></div></div><div class="article_header-footer"><div class="article_header-footer-left"><div class="article_header-links"><input class="pub-doi" type="hidden" name="pub-doi" value="10.1021/acsami.6b15887"><a href="/doi/epdf/10.1021/acsami.6b15887?ref=article_openPDF" title="PDF" target="_blank" class="article__btn__secondary" data-id="article_header_OpenPDF"><i class="icon-open-pdf"></i><span>Open PDF</span></a><a href="#_i8" title="Supporting Information" class="article__btn__secondary internalNav" data-id="article_header_SupportingInfo"><i class="icon-supporting-info"></i><span>Supporting Information (1)</span></a><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=s0&amp;dbid=16384&amp;doi=10.1021/acsami.6b15887&amp;type=refOpenUrl&amp;url=http%3A%2F%2Fimp-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com%2Fopenurl%2F44IMP%2FICL_VU1%3Fid%3Ddoi%3A10.1021%252Facsami.6b15887%26sid%3Dliteratum%253Aachs%26pmid%3D28218513%26genre%3Darticle%26aulast%3DHuang%26date%3D2017%26atitle%3DEnhancing%2BSodium-Ion%2BStorage%2BBehaviors%2Bin%2BTiNb2O7%2Bby%2BMechanical%2BBall%2BMilling%26jtitle%3DACS%2BApplied%2BMaterials%2B%2526%2BInterfaces%26title%3DACS%2BApplied%2BMaterials%2B%2526%2BInterfaces%26volume%3D9%26issue%3D10%26spage%3D8696%26epage%3D8703%26issn%3D1944-8244" title="OpenURL IMPERIAL COLLEGE LONDON" class="issue-item_open-url sfxLink"><img src="/userimages/9157ccea-6c85-4e5f-ac2a-6a9fd9422c17/sfxbutton-180e067c810" alt="OpenURL IMPERIAL COLLEGE LONDON"></a></div></div></div><div class="article_header-meta"><div class="article_header-meta-left"><input type="hidden" name="journalCodeForjhpLink" value="aamick"><input type="hidden" name="journalNameForjhpLink" value="ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces"><div class="article_header-journal"><h2>ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</h2></div><div class="article_header-cite-this" tabindex="0"><input type="hidden" name="citation-copy-value" value="ACS Appl. class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D8696%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYangyang%2BHuang%252C%2BXiang%2BLi%252C%2BJiahuan%2BLuo%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D9%26issueNum%3D10%26contentID%3Dacsami.6b15887%26title%3DEnhancing%2BSodium-Ion%2BStorage%2BBehaviors%2Bin%2BTiNb2O7%2Bby%2BMechanical%2BBall%2BMilling%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D8703%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2017" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/medium/am-2016-15887j_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/large/am-2016-15887j_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b15887&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have shown extensive prospects as alternative rechargeable batteries in large-scale energy storage systems, because of the abundance and low cost of sodium. The development of high-performance cathode and anode materials is a big challenge for SIBs. As is well known, TiNb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (TNO) exhibits a high capacity of ∼250 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with excellent capacity retention as a Li-insertion anode for lithium-ion batteries, but it has rarely been discussed as an anode for SIBs. Here, we demonstrate ball-milled TiNb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (BM-TNO) as an SIB anode, which provides an average voltage of ∼0.6 V and a reversible capacity of ∼180 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 15 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, and presents excellent cyclability with 95% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. A possible Na storage mechanism in BM-TNO is also proposed.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D8696%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYangyang%2BHuang%252C%2BXiang%2BLi%252C%2BJiahuan%2BLuo%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D9%26issueNum%3D10%26contentID%3Dacsami.6b15887%26title%3DEnhancing%2BSodium-Ion%2BStorage%2BBehaviors%2Bin%2BTiNb2O7%2Bby%2BMechanical%2BBall%2BMilling%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D8703%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2017" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Diffusion" class="article__tags__link">Diffusion</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrical%20properties" class="article__tags__link">Electrical properties</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Ions" class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Tinb2o7&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_TiNb2O7">TiNb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na-ion%20batteries">Na-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ball+Milling&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ball%20milling">ball milling</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Capacitive&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_capacitive">capacitive</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na-ion+Diffusion+Coefficient&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na-ion%20diffusion%20coefficient">Na-ion diffusion coefficient</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been extensively used for decades in portable electronics and energy storage systems. Recently, the fast-increasing market demands of LIBs in electric and electric hybrid vehicles <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1, 2)</a> make it urgent to consider the high-cost and limited lithium resources for large-scale applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 3 to 6">(3-6)</a> Therefore, alternative rechargeable batteries with low cost and long cycle life based on abundant resources are desirable. Room-temperature sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have instantly attracted significant research interests, because of their potential advantages, such as low-cost and abundant sodium resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 7 to 19">(7-19)</a> Since the Na atom is larger and heavier than the Li atom, the gravimetric and volumetric energy densities of SIBs would not exceed those of Li analogues. Moreover, the electrode materials for SIBs are different from those used in LIBs. It is necessary to explore and discover new electrode materials with large capacity, long cycle life, and high reliability for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 20 to 25">(20-25)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Transition-metal oxides have been widely investigated as high-capacity anode materials of LIBs for their potential applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Generally, the metal oxide materials store lithium through the following routes:<table class="listgroup" border="0" width="95%" list-type="label"><tbody><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">(1)</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">the metal oxide M<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub><i>y</i></sub> electrochemically reacts with Li to transfer into Li<sub>2</sub>O and metallic M during discharge processes; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a></p></td></tr><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">(2)</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">Li ions are inserted into the lattice vacancy sites (for example, TiO<sub>2</sub> + <i>x</i>Li → Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>TiO<sub>2</sub>); <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> and</p></td></tr><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">(3)</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">Li ions occupy the lattice sites during the insertion, such as Li<sub>3<i>x</i></sub>La<sub>(2/3)–<i>x</i></sub>□<sub>(1/3)–2<i>x</i></sub>TiO<sub>3</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 28 and 29">(28, 29)</a></p></td></tr></tbody></table>Among the investigated metal oxide materials, TiNb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (TNO) with a monoclinic structure has been recently developed as a LIB anode material; this was first reported by Goodenough’s group in 2011. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> It exhibits two-dimensional (2D) ionic channels that favor Li-ion mobility, leading to high rate capability. Furthermore, it exhibits a high theoretical specific capacity (∼387 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) with a five-electron transfer reaction, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 30 to 34">(30-34)</a> showing a safer potential plateau.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Recently, much effort has been devoted to improving the electrochemical performance through design of the microstructure, such as micrometer TNO <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> and nanoscale TNO. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 35 to 37">(35-37)</a> The nanostructures can greatly shorten the ion migration and diffusion paths, hence improving the electrochemical properties in LIBs and SIB. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The above results show that the volume expansion of TNO during lithiation is much lower than that of alloy electrodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39, 40)</a> As reported, the excellent electrochemical properties have been achieved via design of disordered or amorphous electrode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41, 42)</a> Combined with the above discussion, we are keen to know whether TNO can also serve as anode material for SIBs. It would be valuable to investigate the wonderful difference between the lithiation and sodiation in TNO. One can modify the particle size and morphologies of the electrode material and, hence, adjust the electrode interface. We have compared the sodium storage performances between pristine TNO and BM-TNO and found superior Na-ion storage behavior in BM-TNO. A possible mechanism is proposed.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3"> Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">TiNb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> was synthesized via a traditional solid-state reaction method. Stoichiometric amounts of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> were thoroughly mixed together in a mortar and pestle. The obtained powder was calcined in air at 1100 °C for 12 h. The ball milling was performed in a high-speed (1400 rpm/min) ball mill (MSK-SFM-3). Steel balls with different diameters (12, 6, and 5 mm in a ratio of 1:2:3) were mixed with the as-prepared TNO powder in a ball-to-powder weight ratio of 13:1. The samples were milled for 0.5, 2, 4, and 6 h, respectively.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The phase of the samples was identified by using an X-ray diffraction (XRD) system (PANalytical X’pert PRO MRO, Holland) that applied Cu Kα radiation. The morphologies were observed by field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) using an FEI-Sirion 200 system that was coupled with an EDX spectrometer. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM), and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) images were observed with a JEOL Model JEM-2010F microscope.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Electrochemical Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">A well-mixed slurry was produced by mixing as-prepared samples with sodium carboxymethyl cellulose (Na-CMC) and Super P, in a weight ratio of 7:2:1, in deionized water and then was coated on a copper foil and dried under vacuum at 90 °C for 12 h. Typical coin cells (CR2032) were assembled in an Ar-filled glovebox. For SIBs, sodium metal foil was used as the counter electrode, and glass fiber was used as the separator. A solution of 1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> solution with a mixed solvent of ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) (v/v = 1:1) was used as the electrolyte. For LIBs, Li foil served as the counter electrode, Celgrad acted as the separator, and 1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> LiPF<sub>6</sub> solution in EC/DEC (v/v = 1:1) was used as the electrolyte. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was performed on an electrochemistry workstation (PMC-500) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> within a voltage range of 0.01–2.5 V. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were achieved at an electrochemistry workstation (Model PMC-500) within a frequency range between 0.01 Hz and 10 kHz. Galvanostatic discharge–charge tests were executed on a Land battery measurement system at various current densities within a voltage range of 0.01–2.5 V at constant temperature. Galvanostatic intermittent titration (GITT) measurements were also conducted on Land battery measurement system; the cells were discharged/charged at a current density of 15 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with a current pulse duration of 30 min and relaxation time of 60 min.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Pristine TNO was prepared via a simple solid-state reaction with stoichiometric Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and TiO<sub>2</sub> as starting materials by sintering at 1100 °C in air for 12 h. By examining the XRD pattern, the as-prepared TNO is determined to be a pure phase with monoclinic structure (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/suppl_file/am6b15887_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> in the Supporting Information). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Rietveld refinement using a monoclinic space group <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> (<i>Z</i> = 6) gives lattice parameters of <i>a</i> = 20.351(3) Å, <i>b</i> = 3.801(2) Å, <i>c</i> = 11.882(2) Å, and β = 120.19(1)°. The crystal structure of TNO along the <i>b</i>-axis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/suppl_file/am6b15887_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> in the Supporting Information) shows that a basic octahedral unit is formed by one coordinated metal atom (Ti/Nb) and six O atoms, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 43 to 45">(43-45)</a> and then the octahedra connect by corner-sharing and edge-sharing to form a crystallographic framework.</div><div class="NLM_p">The BM-TNO samples were obtained by ball milling the pristine TNO for 0.5, 2, 4, and 6 h; these samples are denoted BM-TNO-0.5, BM-TNO-2, BM-TNO-4, and BM-TNO-6, respectively. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows the XRD patterns. With increased ball-milling time, the diffraction peaks become broader and the full width at half-maximum (fwhm) increases remarkably, revealing the decrease of crystallinity and particle size. Many peaks of pristine TNO, such as (3̅03), cannot be observed after ball milling. It is observed that those new peaks appear in pairs that are close to the vanished peaks, such as (3̅03) and (101). We assume that the new peaks represent the disorder and distorted parts in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figures 1">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e, as a result of the ball milling on the exposed lattice face. The BM-TNO-6 sample almost shows different diffractions from pristine TNO, and the peak intensity greatly decreases, which demonstrates that the BM-TNO-6 becomes amorphous. This indicates that the ball-milling process can result in the formation of a disordered phase.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/medium/am-2016-15887j_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/large/am-2016-15887j_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD patterns of BM-TNOs; (b) TEM image of as-prepared TNO (inset shows an SEM image); (c) HR-TEM image of as-prepared TNO with (105) crystal planes marked; (d) TEM image of BM-TNO-4 (inset shows an enlarged view); and (e) HR-TEM of BM-TNO-4, showing crystalline parts with ordered and disordered domains and amorphous domains coexist in an individual particle of BM-TNO-6.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/large/am-2016-15887j_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b15887&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphologies of the pristine TNO and typical BM-TNO-4 sample were observed by SEM and HR-TEM. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/suppl_file/am6b15887_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> in the Supporting Information shows SEM images of BM-TNO-<i>n</i> samples, in which a downsizing at the nanolevel by the collision shock between the steel balls and the inner wall of the jar is presented. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b, the particle size of the pristine TNO distributes evenly, ranging from 2 μm to 3 μm. The HR-TEM image clearly shows lattice fringes of nanocrystallites with a regular spacing of ∼0.23 nm, which is consistent with the interplanar distance of (105) planes of TNO. The HR-TEM images of the BM-TNO-4 in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/suppl_file/am6b15887_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> in the Supporting Information reveal the formation of nanocrystals and dislocations, clearly indicating the structural change from crystalline to disordered phase induced by ball milling. Moreover, the element analysis results (EDX) in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/suppl_file/am6b15887_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> in the Supporting Information show no Fe impurity phase in BM-TNOs samples. In addition, a series of BM-TNO-<i>n</i> samples with different specific surface areas are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/suppl_file/am6b15887_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> in the Supporting Information. BM-TNO-4 shows the maximum specific surface area, compared with other samples. When prolonging the ball milling time (<i>n</i> = 6), BM-TNO-6 shows a smaller specific surface area than BM-TNO-4, suggesting a more obvious agglomeration of nanoparticles.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a presents the fourth discharge and charge voltage profiles of the pristine TNO and BM-TNO-4 tested as anodes in SIB anodes within a potential window of 0.01–2.5 V. The reversible capacities of TNO and BM-TNO-4 are 20 and 175 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to ∼0.05 and ∼0.5 Na ions per formula unit insert into the host, respectively. The BM-TNO-4 shows not only a high reversible capacity, but also an excellent cyclability, with 96% capacity retention after 20 cycles at a current density of 15 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Whereas, the pristine TNO exhibits poor electrochemical performance, with a capacity of &lt;20 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 20 cycles. This means that the pristine TNO is almost electrochemically inert vs Na/Na<sup>0</sup>, but BM-TNO is a more effective anode material for SIBs. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurement was carried out to confirm the above results at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.01–2.5 V to confirm the electrochemical activity (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/suppl_file/am6b15887_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a> in the Supporting Information). The first sodiated curve of pristine TNO shows two reduction peaks at 0.4 and 0.87 V, which suggest the decomposition of the electrolyte to form solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film. The sharp anodic and cathodic peaks at ∼0.05 and ∼0.09 V, respectively, agree well with redox peaks of active carbon (Super P) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/suppl_file/am6b15887_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a> in the Supporting Information), which cannot be assigned to TNO.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/medium/am-2016-15887j_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/large/am-2016-15887j_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Fourth discharge/charge profiles of pristine TNO and BM-TNO-4 for SIBs at a current density of 15 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (b) cycling performance at a current density of 15 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (c) rate performance at current densities from 15 mA g<sup>–1</sup> to 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>; (d) long-term cycling performance of ball-milled TNO at a current rate of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (e) discharge/charge profiles of pristine TNO and BM-TNO-4 for LIBs at a current density of 38.7 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; and (f) long-term cycling performance of pristine TNO and BM-TNO-4 for LIBs at a current density of 387 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/large/am-2016-15887j_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b15887&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c shows the rate performance of BM-TNO anodes in SIBs at various current densities from 15 mA g<sup>–1</sup> to 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The BM-TNO-4 anode demonstrates the best comprehensive performance. The specific capacities are 163, 117, 101, 79, and 60 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at current densities of 15, 50, 100, 200, and 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/suppl_file/am6b15887_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a> in the Supporting Information shows that the overpotential resulting from different current rates are rather symmetrical for sodiation/desodiation. The electronic and ionic resistances in the system that cause the cutoff voltage are rapidly reached at higher rates, leading to reductions in capacity. The Coulombic efficiency (CE) is ∼100% after an initial CE of 61% (recall <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/suppl_file/am6b15887_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>) at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Importantly, the BM-TNO-6h anode gives the largest initial capacity at low current density, which reaches 240 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 15 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. This can be ascribed to the highest degree of disorder that is created by long-time ball milling. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d shows the cycle performance of BM-TNO anodes in SIBs at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. We can still see that the initial capacity of BM-TNO-6 is highest, but quickly decreases. Compared to BM-TNO-6, BM-TNO-4 exhibits relatively lower initial capacity but much better cycle stability, with a capacity retention of ∼95% after 500 cycles. The experiments evidently indicate that the Na-ion storage property is strongly dependent on the ball-milling time. Essentially, different milling times result in different degrees of disorder in the structure of TNO. High disorder is favorable for Na-ion storage, but exorbitant disorder is disadvantageous to stable cycling performance. The pristine TNO, by contrast, shows a negligible reversible Na-storage capacity. BM-TNO-6 may show smaller crystalline size and large surface area than expected, which is consistent with the high initial specific capacity observed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d. However, it does not provide more stable cycle performance as usual. In contrast, exorbitant disorder caused inferior cycle performance, which is also mentioned in a recent report on mechanically ball-milled silicon as the anode for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">In order to understand the essential difference in electrochemical performance for the ordered and disordered TNO phases, we compared the Li-ion storage behaviors between pristine TNO and BM-TNO-4. The charge/discharge measurements were carried out with TNO as BM-TNO as anode materials for LIBs in a voltage range of 1.0–3.0 V. The typical discharge and charge profiles are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e. The pristine TNO exhibits an obvious plateau at 1.6 V versus Li<sup>+</sup>/Li<sup>0</sup> and a reversible capacity of 240 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 38.7 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. However, the BM-TNO-4 anode exhibits slope discharge/charge curves without obvious plateaus and much lower capacity (140 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) than pristine TNO. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f displays their cyclic stability evaluated at 1 C for 200 cycles. Although both of them exhibit good electrochemical stability, BM-TNO-4 only gives low capacity. In addition, the initial columbic efficiency of BM-TNO-4 is also lower than pristine TNO. Interesting, the case in Li-storage is very different from that in Na-storage for TNO and BM-TNO. A possible explanation might be that the energy storage mechanisms in LIBs and SIBs for TNO and BM-TNO are decidedly different. The ball milling creates many inserted nanocrystals and an amorphous phase in the TNO sample, which should reduce lithiation active sites in TNO crystal structure, but conversely increase sodiation active sites. The increase in the amount of amorphous phase by ball milling limits the effective Li-ion insertion paths. In addition, the ball milling introduced a greater amount of fresh surface exposed to Li-ion organic electrolyte, which prevents an effective formation of SEI film on the surfaces of BM-TNO-4 particles, and, hence, results in high irreversible capacity, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e.</div><div class="NLM_p">Based on the above results, we attempt to recap the sodium and lithium storage mechanism for the pristine TNO and BM-TNO. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>, since the Na<sup>+</sup> ion is larger than the Li<sup>+</sup> ion, a small amount of Na<sup>+</sup> ions could only be trapped on the surface of pristine TNO, instead of within lattice sites during the sodiation process. However, Li<sup>+</sup> ions can be inserted into the lattice sites of pristine TNO during the lithiation processes, achieving a high reversible capacity. By way of contrast, there are many nanocrystals, dislocations, and microfissures in BM-TNO anodes, which can take plenty of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, but with a certain amount of irreversible capacity.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/medium/am-2016-15887j_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/large/am-2016-15887j_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Schematic illustration of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and Li<sup>+</sup> ions intercalating into TNO and BM-TNO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/large/am-2016-15887j_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b15887&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">For insight into the mechanism of sodiation/desodiation process of in BM-TNO-4 electrode, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was used to measure the Na-ion diffusion coefficient. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figures 4">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b show the GITT curves of BM-TNO-4 anode at the first and second cycle from 0.01 to 2.5 V. During the GITT tests, the sodiation and desodiation were carried out at a constant current density of 15 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for an interval of 30 min, followed by an open-circuit stand for value (<i>E</i><sub>s</sub>).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/medium/am-2016-15887j_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/large/am-2016-15887j_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. GITT sodiation/desodiation tests of (a) the initial cycle and (b) the second cycle at a current pulse of 15 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for 30 min during discharge/charge and relaxation for 1 h. <i>E</i> vs <i>t</i> profiles for a single step of a GITT experiment of BM-TNO-4 anode: (c) first sodiation process and (d) second sodiation process. (f) Diffusion coefficients calculated from the GITT potential profiles in (e) the first sodiation/desodiation process and (f) the second sodiation/desodiation process.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/large/am-2016-15887j_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b15887&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Single titration profiles of the first and second sodiation process are illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figures 4">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d with schematic labeling of different parameters. The observed voltage change Δ<i>E</i> is obtained by subtracting the original voltage (<i>E</i><sub>0</sub>) from the steady-state voltage (<i>E</i><sub>s</sub>). Combining with <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figures 4">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, we find that the overpotential at low voltage is lower than that at high voltage in sodiation/desodiation processes. Furthermore, the overpotential increases slightly in the second cycle, which agrees well with cycling stability of BM-TNO-4 (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d). The ionic diffusion coefficient of Na ions in BM-TNO-4 electrode can be determined by solving the Fick’s second law with the following equation:<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/am-2016-15887j_m001.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></span>where <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> is the electrode active mass, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> the molecular mass of BM-TNO-4, <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> the molar volume of the material, <i>S</i> the overall contract area of the electrode and electrolyte, <i>L</i> the electrode thickness, and τ the charging time. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/suppl_file/am6b15887_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S10a and S10b</a> in the Supporting Information, the voltage shows a linear relationship with the square root of τ. Therefore, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a> can be further simplified to<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/am-2016-15887j_m002.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></span></div><div class="NLM_p">Based on <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>, the evolving diffusion coefficients of BM-TNO-4 as a function of cell voltage is plotted in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figures 4">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e shows that the sodium-ion chemical diffusion coefficient in first discharge process varies from 0.9 × 10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> to 0.2 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>. The result of discharge section have a similar rule to the initial discharge curve. The chemical diffusion coefficient decreases slowly as the voltage decreases, reaching a minimum at ∼0.24 V, then increases slightly and decreases again when the voltage reaches the cutoff voltage. During the charge process, the diffusion coefficient varies from 0.3 × 10<sup>–11</sup> to 1.5 × 10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> and the curve exhibits a twisting downtrend. The diffusion coefficient in the second discharge is higher than that of the initial discharge, but the curves is similar to the initial cycle. In addition, coinciding with the CV results, GITT there displays a slightly lower diffusion coefficient at the minimum charge.</div><div class="NLM_p">The better rate performance of BM-TNO-4 can be ascribed to its quick charge transfer kinetics of Faradaic reactions, as revealed by the electrochemical impedance spectra (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>). The semicircle in the high-middle frequency range represents charge-transfer resistance between the electrolyte and electrode, and the low-frequency line response represents the Warburg resistance related to the Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion. Based on the fitted equivalent circuit in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>, the charge-transfer resistance is simulated to be ∼700 and ∼42 Ω, respectively, which suggests that ball-milling makes the resistance smaller and also enables charging/discharging measurements at high current density.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/medium/am-2016-15887j_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/large/am-2016-15887j_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Comparison of electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) plots for pristine TNO and BM-TNO-4.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/large/am-2016-15887j_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b15887&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The CV technique was further employed to investigate the Na-ion storage mechanism in BM-TNO-4. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a shows CV curves in the range of 0.01–2.5 V at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. A broad cathodic peak at ∼0.4 V in the initial cycle is observed due to the formation of SEI on the fresh surface of the active material particles. In the subsequent cycles, the CV curves are overlapped with long cathodic slopes, which agrees with the cycling performance, showing excellent reversibility. It demonstrates that the Na<sup>+</sup>-ion storage in the amorphous parts and/or nanocrystals is mainly featured by a capacitive behavior in the full voltage window. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47, 48)</a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b shows that CV measurements were performed at different scan rates to evaluate the rate behavior. At high scan rates, the CV curves separately shift from the standard position, because of the increased polarizations and Ohmic resistance. On the other hand, according to the relationship between the measured current (<i>i</i>) and the scan rate (<i>v</i>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/am-2016-15887j_m003.gif" alt=""></span>the <i>b</i>-value can be calculated by the slope of the log(<i>v</i>)–log(<i>i</i>) plots. If <i>b</i> = 0.5, the electrochemical behavior is totally controlled by diffusion process; if <i>b</i> = 1.0, the behavior is a capacitive process. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c shows the relationship between the scan rates and peak current. The slope is 0.87, based on the CV curves from 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> to 3 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b), indicating that the sodium storage behavior in the BM-TNO-4 is mainly a pseudo-capacitive process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref50" aria-label="reference 47 and 50">(47, 50)</a> As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d, the CV curves at the scan rate of 0.8 mV s<sup>–1</sup> show that the capacitive capacity is ∼78.1% of the total capacity. Therefore, the results suggest that the BM-TNO materials that are electrochemically active, relative to the Na<sup>+</sup> ion, have a great effect on the capacitive process. The great number of nanocrystals and high surface areas caused by ball milling are propitious to the capacitive effect.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/medium/am-2016-15887j_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/large/am-2016-15887j_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) CV curves of the first to fifth cycle at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) CV curves from 0.1 to 10 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Relationship between the peak currents and scan rates to determine the <i>b</i>-value. (d) Separation of the capacitive and diffusion charge for the BM-TNO-4 anode at a scan rate of 0.8 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/asset/images/large/am-2016-15887j_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b15887&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, TiNb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (TNO) in different states have been evaluated as the anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). The pristine TNO prepared by solid-state reaction shows low activity to Na<sup>+</sup>-ion storage, whereas the disordered ball-milled TiNb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (BM-TNO) materials exhibit high Na<sup>+</sup>-ion storage performance. TEM and XRD analysis show that BM-TNOs consist of a great number of nanocrystals surrounded by an amorphous phase. The BM-TNO-4 exhibits the best electrochemical performance as an anode in SIBs. Moreover, the remarkable different electrochemical behaviors of the sodiation and lithiation processes in BM-TNO-4 were systematically investigated, and the results showed that ball milling is an efficient method to modify the dynamic process of the electrode material for SIBs. The kinetics analysis based on CV reveals that Na-ion storage in BM-TNOs is mainly dominated by capacitive behavior, which is confirmed by the results of GITT tests. The research results show that the sodium-ion storage behaviors of TNO were evidently enhanced, because of the introduction of nanocrystals and high surface areas created by ball milling. Our findings have provided an effective strategy to search for high-performance electrode materials for SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.6b15887" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.6b15887</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">X-ray diffraction and Rietveld refinement of TNO (Figure S1); schematic structure of TNO (Figure S2); SEM images of BM-TNOs (Figure S3); TEM and HR-TEM images of BM-TNO-4 (Figure S4); EDX analysis of the BM-TNO-4 sample (Figure S5); the initial sodiation/desodiation curve of BM-TNO-4 (Figure S6); CV profiles of pristine TNO (Figure S7) and active carbon (Super P) (Figure S8); Voltage response of first cycle discharge/charge curves (Figure S9); first and second sodiation processes for the <i>E</i> vs <i>t</i> profile for a single step of a GITT experiment of BM-TNO-4 anode (Figure S10); results of N<sub>2</sub> adsorption measurement on all samples (Table S1) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/suppl_file/am6b15887_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b15887/suppl_file/am6b15887_si_001.pdf">am6b15887_si_001.pdf (1.12 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b15887" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">
