        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of the development of lithium-based rechargeable batteries.  Ongoing research strategies are highlighted, and the challenges that remain regarding the synthesis, characterization, electrochem. performance, and safety of these systems are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGowMxv9DmkLwrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgSFQk-G9Xpmw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3MXovFGitrY%253D&amp;md5=944485672a9bdf09f6e6a7a199bf3d43</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, S. W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Seo, D. H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ma, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ceder, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kang, K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrode Materials for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries: Potential Alternatives to Current Lithium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. Energy Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">2</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">710</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">721</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.201200026</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D2%26publication_year%3D2012%26pages%3D710-721%26journal%3DAdv.%2BEnergy%2BMater.%26author%3DS.%2BW.%2BKim%26author%3DD.%2BH.%2BSeo%26author%3DX.%2BMa%26author%3DG.%2BCeder%26author%3DK.%2BKang%26title%3DElectrode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BRechargeable%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BPotential%2BAlternatives%2Bto%2BCurrent%2BLithium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Faenm.201200026&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.6b15887&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Faenm.201200026&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">710-721</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGonnrfiSUpaRrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgSFQk-G9Xpmw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVOrtLjF&amp;md5=485b20393bb8876b2f69ca708aeba606</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1038/nmat2007"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ellis, B.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In the search for new pos.-electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries, recent research has focused on nanostructured lithium transition-metal phosphates that exhibit desirable properties such as high energy storage capacity combined with electrochem. stability.  Only one member of this class-the olivine LiFePO4 (ref. 3)-has risen to prominence so far, owing to its other characteristics, which include low cost, low environmental impact and safety.  These are crit. for large-capacity systems such as plug-in hybrid elec. vehicles.  Nonetheless, olivine has some inherent shortcomings, including 1-dimensional lithium-ion transport and a two-phase redox reaction that together limit the mobility of the phase boundary.  Thus, nanocrystallites are key to enable fast rate behavior.  Also probably the long-term economic viability of large-scale Li-ion energy storage systems could be ultimately limited by global lithium reserves, although this remains speculative at present.  Here, the authors report on a sodium/lithium iron phosphate, A2FePO4F (A = Na, Li), that could serve as a cathode in either Li-ion or Na-ion cells.  Also, it possesses facile two-dimensional pathways for Li+ transport, and the structural changes on redn.-oxidn. are minimal.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljB7sow_kiwIA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1039/c0ee00777c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zu, C.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Flexible and Binder-Free Electrodes of Sb/rGO and Na3V2(PO4)3/rGO Nanocomposites for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Wei; Liu, Yangtao; Chen, Chaoji; Li, Zhen; Huang, Yunhui; Hu, Xianluo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Flexible power sources showed great promise in next-generation bendable, implantable, and wearable electronic systems.  Here, flexible and binder-free electrodes of Na3V2(PO4)3/reduced graphene oxide (NVP/rGO) and Sb/rGO nanocomposites for Na-ion batteries are reported.  The Sb/rGO and NVP/rGO paper electrodes with high flexibility and tailorability can be easily fabricated.  Sb and NVP nanoparticles are embedded homogeneously in the interconnected framework of rGO nanosheets, which provides structurally stable hosts for Na-ion intercalation and deintercalation.  The NVP/rGO paper-like cathode delivers a reversible capacity of 113 mA h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 and high capacity retention of ≈96.6% after 120 cycles.  The Sb/rGO paper-like anode gives a highly reversible capacity of 612 mA h g-1 at 100 mA g-1, an excellent rate capacity up to 30 C, and a good cycle performance.  Also, the Na-ion full cell of NVP/rGO//Sb/rGO was fabricated, delivering a highly reversible capacity of ≈400 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 after 100 charge/discharge cycles.  This work may provide promising electrode candidates for developing next-generation energy-storage devices with high capacity and long cycle life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqEuCL3VQtFtbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljB7sow_kiwIA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXnsFCltrw%253D&amp;md5=c6e885e922194d560b18ff20c7794ae7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms7929"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wen, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yan, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mai, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shan, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Na<sup>+</sup> Intercalation Pseudocapacitance in Graphene-Coupled Titanium Oxide Enabling Ultra-Fast Sodium Storage and Long-Term Cycling</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are emerging as a highly promising technol. for large-scale energy storage applications.  However, it remains a significant challenge to develop an anode with superior long-term cycling stability and high-rate capability.  Here we demonstrate that the Na+ intercalation pseudocapacitance in TiO2/graphene nanocomposites enables high-rate capability and long cycle life in a sodium-ion battery.  This hybrid electrode exhibits a specific capacity of above 90 mA h g-1 at 12,000 mA g-1 (∼36 C).  The capacity is highly reversible for more than 4,000 cycles, the longest demonstrated cyclability to date.  First-principle calcns. demonstrate that the intimate integration of graphene with TiO2 reduces the diffusion energy barrier, thus enhancing the Na+ intercalation pseudocapacitive process.  The Na-ion intercalation pseudocapacitance enabled by tailor-deigned nanostructures represents a promising strategy for developing electrode materials with high power d. and long cycle life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrGrV7K8Tq5mLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljB7sow_kiwIA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtF2lurzK&amp;md5=99303fd99ef59044874690750282067a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201100655"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qian, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nanosized Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>/C Composite as a Low-Cost and High-Rate Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium and sodium-ion energy storage batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ellis, Brian L.; Nazar, Linda F.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Current Opinion in Solid State &amp; Materials Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">16</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Owing to almost unmatched volumetric energy d., Li-ion batteries have dominated the portable electronics industry and solid state electrochem. literature for the past 20 years.  Not only will that continue, but they are also now powering plug-in hybrid elec. vehicles and elec. vehicles.  In light of possible concerns over rising lithium costs in the future, Na and Na-ion batteries have re-emerged as candidates for medium and large-scale stationary energy storage, esp. as a result of heightened interest in renewable energy sources that provide intermittent power which needs to be load-levelled.  The sodium-ion battery field presents many solid state materials design challenges, and rising to that call in the past couple of years, several reports of new sodium-ion technologies and electrode materials have surfaced.  These range from high-temp. air electrodes to new layered oxides, polyanion-based materials, carbons and other insertion materials for sodium-ion batteries, many of which hold promise for future sodium-based energy storage applications.  In this article, the challenges of current high-temp. sodium technologies including Na-S and Na-NiCl2 and new molten sodium technol., Na-O2 are summarized.  Recent advancements in pos. and neg. electrode materials suitable for Na-ion and hybrid Na/Li-ion cells are reviewed, along with the prospects for future developments.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGri5CIY1TtTi7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgWW_xH-LJmvg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XntVWisLc%253D&amp;md5=3f29c1865dee5ad932049567fa6f5b68</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1039/c2ee22864e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Abouimrane, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Weng, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Eltayeb, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cui, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Niklas, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Poluektov, O.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Amine, K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium Insertion in Carboxylate Based Materials and Their Application in 3.6 V Full Sodium Cells</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Energy Environ.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium insertion in carboxylate based materials and their application in 3.6 V full sodium cells</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Abouimrane, Ali; Weng, Wei; Eltayeb, Hussameldin; Cui, Yanjie; Niklas, Jens; Poluektov, Oleg; Amine, Khalil</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The sodium battery has the potential to be the next generation rechargeable system which utilizes cheaper and more abundant sodium material but affords nearly the same power as lithium batteries.  One of the key barriers for the sodium battery is the lack of stable anode materials which can insert sodium ions reversibly at relatively low potential.  This contribution reports the sodium insertion in a series of org. carboxylate based materials: (C8H4Na2O4), (C8H6O4), (C8H5NaO4), (C8Na2F4O4), (C10H2Na4O8), (C14H4O6) and (C14H4Na4O8) at low voltage (below 0.6 V vs. Na/Na+).  These org. anode materials can insert reversibly up to 2 Na per mol. with good cycleability.  The Na insertion mechanism was proposed and 3.6 V full sodium batteries were made and cycled reversibly at room temp. and at 55 °C.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqohlWV-41Oy7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgWW_xH-LJmvg"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-rich prussian blue analogs (PBs) with high coulombic efficiency are of fundamental and technol. importance for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Here we report high-quality NaxFeFe(CN)6 nanocubes as cathode materials for SIBs and investigate their sodium storage mechanism.  Among them, Na-rich Na1.70FeFe(CN)6 shows highly reversible electrochem. reactions, delivering a capacity as high as 120.7 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1; even at 1200 mA g-1, the capacity still retains up to 73.6 mA h g-1.  The first-cycle coulombic efficiency strongly depends on the sodium content in NaxFeFe(CN)6.  Exptl. results and first-principle calcns. demonstrate that sodium cations in the large cavities of PBs have a priority to occupy the 8c site, while in the Na-rich samples, Na+ ions can be pushed into other 24d site.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Slater, Michael D.; Kim, Donghan; Lee, Eungje; Doeff, Marca; Johnson, Christopher S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojsFXLPEQzILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh5_wtywYG5_w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XntlSnurc%253D&amp;md5=fa5d7d089d261a40896cb6b03e88bfbe</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms1843"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hayashi, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Noi, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sakuda, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tatsumisago, M.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Superionic Glass-Ceramic Electrolytes for Room-Temperature Rechargeable Sodium Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat. All-solid-state batteries with inorganic solid electrolytes and                 electrodes are promising power sources for a wide range of applications because of                 their safety, long-cycle lives and versatile geometries.  Rechargeable sodium                 batteries are more suitable than lithium-ion batteries, because they use abundant                 and ubiquitous sodium sources.  Solid electrolytes are critical for realizing                 all-solid-state sodium batteries.  Here we show that stabilization of a                 high-temperature phase by crystallization from the glassy state dramatically                 enhances the Na(+) ion conductivity.  An ambient temperature                 conductivity of over 10(-4) S cm(-1)                 was obtained in a glass-ceramic electrolyte, in which a cubic                     Na(3)PS(4) crystal with superionic conductivity was first                 realized.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Voltage, stability and diffusion barrier differences between sodium-ion and lithium-ion intercalation materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ong, Shyue Ping; Chevrier, Vincent L.; Hautier, Geoffroy; Jain, Anubhav; Moore, Charles; Kim, Sangtae; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3680-3688</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">To evaluate the potential of Na-ion batteries, we contrast in this work the difference between Na-ion and Li-ion based intercalation chemistries in terms of three key battery properties-voltage, phase stability and diffusion barriers.  The compds. investigated comprise the layered AMO2 and AMS2 structures, the olivine and maricite AMPO4 structures, and the NASICON A3V2(PO4)3 structures.  The calcd. Na voltages for the compds. investigated are 0.18-0.57 V lower than that of the corresponding Li voltages, in agreement with previous exptl. data.  We believe the obsd. lower voltages for Na compds. are predominantly a cathodic effect related to the much smaller energy gain from inserting Na into the host structure compared to inserting Li.  We also found a relatively strong dependence of battery properties on structural features.  In general, the difference between the Na and Li voltage of the same structure, ΔVNa-Li, is less neg. for the maricite structures preferred by Na, and more neg. for the olivine structures preferred by Li.  The layered compds. have the most neg. ΔVNa-Li.  In terms of phase stability, we found that open structures, such as the layered and NASICON structures, that are better able to accommodate the larger Na+ ion generally have both Na and Li versions of the same compd.  For the close-packed AMPO4 structures, our results show that Na generally prefers the maricite structure, while Li prefers the olivine structure, in agreement with previous exptl. work.  We also found surprising evidence that the barriers for Na+ migration can potentially be lower than that for Li+ migration in the layered structures.  Overall, our findings indicate that Na-ion systems can be competitive with Li-ion systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGosFyTQ7K1B3LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh5_wtywYG5_w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXht1CqsLzK&amp;md5=5a6e72a3ae5ebf92d84322240b2c29cc</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms2878"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pan, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, Y.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Direct atomic-scale confirmation of three-phase storage mechanism in Li4Ti5O12 anodes for room-temperature sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun Yang; Zhao Liang; Pan Huilin; Lu Xia; Gu Lin; Hu Yong-Sheng; Li Hong; Armand Michel; Ikuhara Yuichi; Chen Liquan; Huang Xuejie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1870</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temperature sodium-ion batteries attract increasing attention for large-scale energy storage applications in renewable energy and smart grid.  However, the development of suitable anode materials remains a challenging issue.  Here we demonstrate that the spinel Li4Ti5O12, well-known as a 'zero-strain' anode for lithium-ion batteries, can also store sodium, displaying an average storage voltage of 0.91 V.  With an appropriate binder, the Li4Ti5O12 electrode delivers a reversible capacity of 155 mAh g(-1) and presents the best cyclability among all reported oxide-based anode materials.  Density functional theory calculations predict a three-phase separation mechanism, 2Li4Ti5O12+6Na(+)+6e(-)↔Li7Ti5O12+Na6LiTi5O12, which has been confirmed through in situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction and advanced scanning transmission electron microscope imaging techniques.  The three-phase separation reaction has never been seen in any insertion electrode materials for lithium- or sodium-ion batteries.  Furthermore, interfacial structure is clearly resolved at an atomic scale in electrochemically sodiated Li4Ti5O12 for the first time via the advanced electron microscopy.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcQPgY8GEu_hzRKDYG3bNImrfW6udTcc2ebFfv0qNlS4obntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC3snlvFClsQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=1ba227dbac0d64af21c548092cc2eb4c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501436"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Swaans, E.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Basak, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zandbergen, H.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Size-Dependent Sodium Storage Mechanism in Li4Ti5O12 Investigated by a Novel Characterization Technique Combining in Situ X-ray Diffraction and Chemical Sodiation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yu, Xiqian; Pan, Huilin; Wan, Wang; Ma, Chao; Bai, Jianming; Meng, Qingping; Ehrlich, Steven N.; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Yang, Xiao-Qing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel characterization technique using the combination of chem. sodiation and synchrotron based in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) is illustrated.  The power of the technique was demonstrated in elucidating the structure evolution of Li4Ti5O12 upon sodium insertion.  The sodium insertion behavior into Li4Ti5O12 is strongly size dependent.  A solid soln. reaction behavior in a wide range has been revealed during sodium insertion into the nanosized Li4Ti5O12 (∼44 nm), which is quite different from the well-known two-phase reaction of Li4Ti5O12/Li7Ti5O12 system during lithium insertion, and also has not been fully addressed in the literature so far.  On the basis of this in situ expt., the apparent Na+ ion diffusion coeff. (DNa+) of Li4Ti5O12 was estd. in the magnitude of 10-16 cm2 s-1, close to the values estd. by electrochem. method, but 5 order of magnitudes smaller than the Li+ ion diffusion coeff. (DLi+ ∼10-11 cm2 s-1), indicating a sluggish Na+ ion diffusion kinetics in Li4Ti5O12 comparing with that of Li+ ion.  Nanosizing the Li4Ti5O12 will be crit. to make it a suitable anode material for sodium-ion batteries.  The application of this novel in situ chem. sodiation method reported in this work provides a facile way and a new opportunity for in situ structure investigations of various sodium-ion battery materials and other systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGptGQCSOa8Iy7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgS05DO-_aFpQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhsVyls7zO&amp;md5=3505dfbcb7d34078623ff1f1e0f31dba</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b06701"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, G.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Insights into the Effects of Zinc Doping on Structural Phase Transition of P2-Type Sodium Nickel Manganese Oxide Cathodes for High-Energy Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Xuehang; Xu, Gui-Liang; Zhong, Guiming; Gong, Zhengliang; McDonald, Matthew J.; Zheng, Shiyao; Fu, Riqiang; Chen, Zonghai; Amine, Khalil; Yang, Yong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">P2-type sodium nickel manganese oxide-based cathode materials with higher energy densities are prime candidates for applications in rechargeable sodium ion batteries.  A systematic study combining in situ high energy X-ray diffraction (HEXRD), ex situ X-ray absorption fine spectroscopy (XAFS), transmission electron microscopy (TEM), and solid-state NMR (SS-NMR) techniques was carried out to gain a deep insight into the structural evolution of P2-Na0.66Ni0.33-xZnxMn0.67O2 (x = 0, 0.07) during cycling.  In situ HEXRD and ex situ TEM measurements indicate that an irreversible phase transition occurs upon sodium insertion-extn. of Na0.66Ni0.33Mn0.67O2.  Zinc doping of this system results in a high structural reversibility.  XAFS measurements indicate that both materials are almost completely dependent on the Ni4+/Ni3+/Ni2+ redox couple to provide charge/discharge capacity.  SS-NMR measurements indicate that both reversible and irreversible migration of transition metal ions into the sodium layer occurs in the material at the fully charged state.  The irreversible migration of transition metal ions triggers a structural distortion, leading to the obsd. capacity and voltage fading.  Our results allow a new understanding of the importance of improving the stability of transition metal layers.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqyUPPhcNq0kLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgS05DO-_aFpQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xht12jsbvM&amp;md5=f3a446894cd9f89f799beef61f2b49ee</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b09898"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bai, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ni, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Na-Rich Na<sub>3+<i>x</i></sub>V<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>Ni<sub><i>x</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C for Sodium Ion Batteries: Controlling the Doping Site and Improving the Electrochemical Performances</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In order to get an element substituted into Na3V2(PO4)3/C in an appointed V site, the simple sol-gel method is used to design and prep. a series of Na-rich Na3+xV2-xNix(PO4)3/C (x = 0-0.07) compds.  To get a charge balance, the ratio of Na, V, and Ni would be changed if Ni goes into a different site.  Hence, ICP is applied to probe the real stoichiometry of the as-prepd. Na3+xV2-xNix(PO4)3/C (x = 0-0.07).  According to the Na/V ratio from the ICP result, it indicates that Ni2+ goes to a V site, and more Na+ will be introduced into the crystal to keep the charge balance.  In addn., the crystal structure changes are explored by XRD and Rietveld refinement, it is indicated from the results that Ni2+ doping does not destroy the lattice structure of Na3V2(PO4)3.  When applied as Na-storage material, the electrochem. property of all Ni2+ doped Na3+xV2-xNix(PO4)3/C composites have been significantly improved, esp. for the Na3.03V1.97Ni0.03(PO4)3/C sample.  For example, 107.1 mAh g-1 can be obtained at the first cycle; after 100 cycles, the capacity retention is as high as 95.5%.  Moreover, when charging/discharging at a higher rate of 5 C, the capacity still remains 88.9 mAh g-1, displaying good rate performance.  The good electrochem. performance is ascribed to the optimized morphol., stable crystal structure, and improved ionic cond.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpQTZRMrirrTLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgS05DO-_aFpQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhsFKmsLvN&amp;md5=e7562109537468250114c379df720cf5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00482"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Luo, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shen, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bommier, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, H.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Shen, Fei; Bommier, Clement; Zhu, Hongli; Ji, Xiulei; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">231-240</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACHRE4</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The intermittent nature of renewable energy sources, such as solar and wind, calls for sustainable elec. energy storage (EES) technologies for stationary applications.  Li will be simply too rare for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) to be used for large-scale storage purposes.  In contrast, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are highly promising to meet the demand of grid-level storage because Na is truly earth abundant and ubiquitous around the globe.  Furthermore, NIBs share a similar rocking-chair operation mechanism with LIBs, which potentially provides high reversibility and long cycling life.  It would be most efficient to transfer knowledge learned on LIBs during the last three decades to the development of NIBs.  Following this logic, rapid progress has been made in NIB cathode materials, where layered metal oxides and polyanionic compds. exhibit encouraging results.  On the anode side, pure graphite as the std. anode for LIBs can only form NaC64 in NIBs if solvent co-intercalation does not occur due to the unfavorable thermodn.  In fact, it was the utilization of a carbon anode in LIBs that enabled the com. successes.  Anodes of metal-ion batteries det. key characteristics, such as safety and cycling life; thus, it is indispensable to identify suitable anode materials for NIBs.In this Account, we review recent development on anode materials for NIBs.  Due to the limited space, we will mainly discuss carbon-based and alloy-based anodes and highlight progress made in our groups in this field.  We first present what is known about the failure mechanism of graphite anode in NIBs.  We then go on to discuss studies on hard carbon anodes, alloy-type anodes, and org. anodes.  Esp., the multiple functions of natural cellulose that is used as a low-cost carbon precursor for mass prodn. and as a soft substrate for tin anodes are highlighted.  The strategies of minimizing the surface area of carbon anodes for improving the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency are also outlined, where graphene oxide was employed as dehydration agent and 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) was used to unzip wood fiber.  Furthermore, surface modification by at. layer deposition technol. is introduced, where we discover that a thin layer of Al2O3 can function to encapsulate Sn nanoparticles, leading to a much enhanced cycling performance.  We also highlight recent work about the phosphorene/graphene anode, which outperformed other anodes in terms of capacity.  The arom. org. anode is also studied as anode with very high initial sodiation capacity.  Furthermore, electrochem. intercalation of Na ions into reduced graphene oxide is applied for fabricating transparent conductors, demonstrating the great feasibility of Na ion intercalation for optical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGorKnQvVSK3P7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgS05DO-_aFpQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVGksrg%253D&amp;md5=90d2907a2b33fdcf59913255073dd969</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1002/adma.200401101"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gou, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> Nanotubes in Gas Sensor and Lithium-Ion Battery Applications</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium lanthanum titanates: a review</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Stramare, S.; Thangadurai, V.; Weppner, W.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  To date, the highest bulk lithium ion-conducting solid electrolyte is the perovskite (ABO3)-type lithium lanthanum titanate (LLT) Li3xLa(2/3)-x.box.(1/3)-2xTiO3 (0 &lt; x &lt; 0.16) and its related structure materials.  The x ≈ 0.1 member exhibits cond. of 1 × 10-3 S/cm at room temp. with an activation energy of 0.40 eV.  The cond. is comparable to that of commonly used polymer/liq. electrolytes.  The ionic cond. of LLT mainly depends on the size of the A-site ion cation (e.g., La or rare earth, alkali or alk. earth), lithium and vacancy concn., and the nature of the B-O bond.  For example, replacement of La by other rare earth elements with smaller ionic radii than that of La decreases the lithium ion cond., while partial substitution of La by Sr (larger ionic radii than that of La) slightly increases the lithium ion cond.  The high lithium ion cond. of LLT is considered to be due to the large concn. of A-site vacancies, and the motion of lithium by a vacancy mechanism through the wide square planar bottleneck between the A sites.  Probably BO6/TiO6 octahedra tilting facilitate the lithium ion mobility in the perovskite structure.  The actual mechanism of lithium ion conduction is not yet clearly understood.  The authors review the structural properties, elec. cond., and electrochem. characterization of LLT and its related materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpxRiby1Na9jbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lik0gZr8zBBaw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3sXnsVOis74%253D&amp;md5=8ee82143e302d475bd19e63349e2c3be</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1021/jp046062j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nakayama, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Usui, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Uchimoto, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wakihara, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yamamoto, M.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Changes in Electronic Structure upon Lithium Insertion into the A-Site Deficient Perovskite Type Oxides (Li,La)TiO<sub>3</sub></span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Changes in Electronic Structure upon Lithium Insertion into the A-Site Deficient Perovskite Type Oxides (Li,La)TiO3</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nakayama, Masanobu; Usui, Tatsuya; Uchimoto, Yoshiharu; Wakihara, Masataka; Yamamoto, Masahiro</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry B</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Investigation on variation of the electronic structure accompanying the electrochem. lithium insertion into the perovskite type oxide, (Li,La)TiO3, has been carried out by X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS).  During the electrochem. lithium insertion, titanium ion reduced its oxidn. state from Ti4+ to Ti3+, while La3+ does not contribute to the redn. reaction resulting from Ti K-edge and La L3-edge XAS, resp.  Furthermore, O K-edge XAS showed marked spectral changes with electrochem. lithium insertion, indicating the electronic structure around oxide ion affected by lithium insertion reaction.  From the XAS measurement, we have concluded the variation obsd. in O K-edge XAS was related to the strong interaction with inserted Li ion.  To confirm this, first-principles band calcns. were performed for the perovskite structure before and after electrochem. lithium insertion.  The calcd. results showed that the electron originated from inserted Li transferred to neighboring oxide ion locally as well as to Ti ion.                         cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Atomic-scale investigation on lithium storage mechanism in TiNb2O7</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lu, Xia; Jian, Zelang; Fang, Zheng; Gu, Lin; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Chen, Wen; Wang, Zhaoxiang; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Titanium niobium oxide (TiNb2O7) with a monoclinic layered structure has been synthesized by a solid state reaction method as an anode candidate for Li-ion batteries.  The TiNb2O7 electrode shows a lithium storage capacity of 281 mAh g-1 with an initial coulombic efficiency as high as 93% at a c.d. of 30 mA g-1 (ca. 0.1C).  The av. lithium insertion voltage is about 1.64 V vs. Li/Li+ at a voltage range of 0.8-3.0 V.  The electrodes exhibit small voltage hysteresis (c.a. 0.1 V at 30 mA g-1) and good capacity retention. In addn., we demonstrate crystal structures of TiNb2O7 and lithiated TiNb2O7 using advanced spherical-aberration-cor. scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM), to picture the lattice sites occupied by the Li, Ti, Nb and O atoms at at.-scale.                         cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Block Copolymer Directed Ordered Mesostructured TiNb2O7 Multimetallic Oxide Constructed of Nanocrystals as High Power Li-Ion Battery Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jo, Changshin; Kim, Youngsik; Hwang, Jongkook; Shim, Jongmin; Chun, Jinyoung; Lee, Jinwoo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">In order to achieve high-power and -energy anodes operating ≥ 1.0 V (vs Li/Li+), titanium-based materials have been investigated for a long time.  However, theor. low lithium charge capacities of titanium-anodes have required new types of high-capacity anode materials.  As a candidate, TiNb2O7 has attracted much attention due to the high theor. capacity of 387.6 mAh/g.  However, the high formation temp. of the TiNb2O7 phase resulted in large-sized TiNb2O7 crystals, thus resulting in poor rate capability.  Herein, ordered mesoporous TiNb2O7 (denoted as m-TNO) was synthesized by block copolymer assisted self-assembly, and the resulting binary metal oxide was applied as an anode in lithium ion battery.  The nanocrystals (∼15 nm) developed inside the confined pore walls and large pores (∼40 nm) of m-TNO resulted in a short diffusion length for lithium ions/electrons and fast penetration of electrolyte.  As a stable anode, the m-TNO electrode exhibited a high capacity of 289 mAh/g (at 0.1 C) and an excellent rate performance of 162 mAh/g at 20 C and 116 mAh/g at 50 C (= 19.35 A/g) within a potential range of 1.0-3.0 V (vs Li/Li+), which clearly surpasses other Ti-and Nb-based anode materials (TiO2, Li4Ti5O12, Nb2O5, etc.) and previously reported TiNb2O7 materials.                         cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Porosity-Controlled TiNb2O7 Microspheres with Partial Nitridation as A Practical Negative Electrode for High-Power Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Park, Hyunjung; Wu, Hao Bin; Song, Taeseup; Lou, Xiong Wen; Paik, Ungyu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1401945/1-1401945/7</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Titanium niobium oxide (TiNb2O7) has been recognized as a promising anode material for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in view of its potential to operate at high rates with improved safety and high theor. capacity of 387 mAh g-1.  However, it suffers from poor Li+ ion diffusivity and low electronic cond. originated from its wide band gap energy (Eg &gt; 2 eV).  Here, porous TiNb2O7 microspheres (PTNO MSs) are prepd. via a facile solvothermal reaction.  PTNO MSs have a particle size of ≈1.2 μm and controllable pore sizes in the range of 5-35 nm.  Ammonia gas nitridation treatment is conducted on PTNO MSs to introduce conducting Ti1-xNbxN layer on the surface and form nitridated PTNO (NPTNO) MSs.  The porous structure and conducting Ti1-xNbxN layer enhance the transport kinetics assocd. with Li+ ions and electrons, which leads to significant improvement in electrochem. performance.  As a result, the NPTNO electrode shows a high discharge capacity of ≈265 mAh g-1, remarkable rate capability (≈143 mAh g-1 at 100 C) and durable long-term cyclability (≈91% capacity retention over 1000 cycles at 5 C).                         cas_cit38R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">38</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">TiNb2O7/graphene composites as high-rate anode materials for lithium/sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Shuang; Cao, Xi; Schmidt, Charles N.; Xu, Qian; Uchaker, Evan; Pei, Yi; Cao, Guozhong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered TiNb2O7/graphene composites (TNO/G) were synthesized through simple direct dispersion and blending in an aq. solvent followed by a freeze drying process and an annealing treatment under Ar.  The graphene sheets are well sepd. by TiNb2O7 (TNO) nanoparticles, and simultaneously, the TNO particles are uniformly anchored between the graphene sheets.  The combined advantages of graphene sheets and TNO, such as the wt. ratio and layered structure, exhibit tremendous benefits for high rate Li ion capability reaching around 180 mA-h g-1 at a c.d. of 38.7 A g-1 (100C rate). For the study of Na ion capability, as graphene is an active material during the de-sodiation process, a reversible capacity of 340.2 mA-h g-1 was obtained from this TNO/G at a c.d. of 25 mA g-1.  Even at a higher c.d. of 200 mA g-1, a stable capacity of 200 mA-h g-1 can still be obtained.  These findings demonstrate the great potential of TNO/G for Li/Na ion anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpuXYqk6TjEsLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj8l45zimI5tw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XisFWlsrc%253D&amp;md5=fe519d1ea3b34df2f134c539db7ee44c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref39"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">39</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit39" data-doi="10.1021/nn506760p"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The authors have attempted to present an overview of alloy anode technol. from the viewpoint of cell energy.  It was very encouraging to find that many advanced alloys can offer about 15-20% improvement in cell energy as compared to graphite.  Although alloy anode technol. has had false starts, the amt. of progress made every year in this field is astounding.  With proper binder, electrolyte formulation, and coating design, alloys have been shown to cycle hundreds of times in high energy d. full cells.  The authors have found some "high capacity" alloys that will result in a reduced cell energy as compared to conventional graphite coatings.  They also attempt to compare anode materials in a meaningful way by introducing a std. model of a full cell stack, based on a cell design.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo_xs76FV8EfLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj8l45zimI5tw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvFagtr3N&amp;md5=726f2975ef98934079f55efb28091130</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit41" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.6b00205"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Abdellahi, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Urban, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dacek, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ceder, G.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Effect of Cation Disorder on the Average Li Intercalation Voltage of Transition-Metal Oxides</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.                         cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The Effect of Cation Disorder on the Average Li Intercalation Voltage of Transition-Metal Oxides</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Abdellahi, Aziz; Urban, Alexander; Dacek, Stephen; Ceder, Gerbrand</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">28</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3659-3665</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Cation disorder is a phenomenon that is becoming increasingly important for the design of high-energy lithium transition-metal oxide pos. electrodes (LiMO2) for Li-ion batteries.  Disordered Li-excess rocksalts have recently been shown to achieve high reversible capacity, and in operando cation disorder (i.e., disorder induced by electrochem. cycling) has been obsd. in a large class of ordered materials.  Despite the growing importance of cation disorder in the Li-ion battery field, very little is known about the effect of cation disorder on the av. voltage (i.e., energy d.) of lithium transition metal oxides.  In this study, the first-principles methods are used to demonstrate that, depending on the transition metal species, cation disorder can lead to an increase or a decrease of the av. voltage of lithium transition metal oxides.  It is further demonstrate that the Ni3+/4+ redox can be high in disordered compds., so that it may be preceded by oxygen activity.  Finally, rules are established for the voltage evolution of compds. that experience in operando disorder.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqbZqcl7XPZ2LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj8l45zimI5tw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XnsVWjtLY%253D&amp;md5=575d6bc538d461607a5acf570782cce6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1021/cm403951b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gao, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gu, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nie, A.                         cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Direct Evidence of Lithium-Induced Atomic Ordering in Amorphous TiO2 Nanotubes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gao, Qi; Gu, Meng; Nie, Anmin; Mashayek, Farzad; Wang, Chongmin; Odegard, Gregory M.; Shahbazian-Yassar, Reza</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-induced at. ordering in amorphous TiO2 nanomaterials has been obsd., and a reaction mechanisms that contradict the many works in the published literature on the lithiation behavior of these materials was proposed.  The lithiation process was conducted in situ inside an at. resoln. transmission electron microscope.  The observations indicate that the lithiation started with the valence redn. of Ti4+ to Ti3+ leading to a LixTiO2 intercalation compd.  The continued intercalation of Li ions in TiO2 nanotubes triggered an amorphous to cryst. phase transformation.  The crystals were formed as nano-islands and identified to be Li2Ti2O4 with cubic structure (a = 8.375 Å).  The tendency for the formation of these crystals was verified with d. functional theory (DFT) simulations.  The size of the cryst. islands provides a characteristic length scale (∼5 nm) at which the at. bonding configuration has been changed within a short time period.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Redn. of the Ti-Nb oxides follows a common pattern.  TiO2 is eliminated, to form a new phase richer in Ti than the original compd., and Nb(IV) replaced Ti(IV) in the original block structure, which is thereby enriched in Nb.  With TiNb2O7, the second phase is a TiO2-NbO2 solid soln., with the rutile structure, initially with a high Ti content, in equil. with a solid soln. of compn. M3O7 (M = metal), isostructural with TiNb2O7.  At log pO2 (atm) ∼-9.0 this reaches the limiting compn. Ti0.72Nb2.28O7, in equil. with Ti0.56Nb0.44O2.  The M3O7 block structure then transforms into the M12O29 block structure (Ti2Nb10O29-Nb12O29 solid soln.), which rapidly increases in Nb content as redn. continues.  Redn. of Ti2Nb10O29 at O fugacities above log pO2 (atm) = -9.0 forms the M3O7 phase as the Ti-rich phase.  At log pO2 = -9.0, and a compn. about Ti1.6Nb10.4O29, the rutile solid soln. takes over as second phase.                         cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium Ion Insertion in Hollow Carbon Nanowires for Battery Applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cao, Yuliang; Xiao, Lifen; Sushko, Maria L.; Wang, Wei; Schwenzer, Birgit; Xiao, Jie; Nie, Zimin; Saraf, Laxmikant V.; Yang, Zhengguo; Liu, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3783-3787</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hollow carbon nanowires were prepd. by pyrolysis of a hollow polyaniline nanowire precursor.  The hollow carbon nanowires used as anode material for Na-ion batteries deliver a high reversible capacity of 251 mA-h/g and 82.2% capacity retention over 400 charge-discharge cycles between 1.2 and 0.01 V (vs. Na+/Na) at a const. current of 50 mA/g (0.2C).  Excellent cycling stability is also obsd. at an even higher charge-discharge rate.  A high reversible capacity of 149 mA-h/g also can be obtained at a current rate of 500 mA/g (2C).  The good Na-ion insertion property is attributed to the short diffusion distance in the hollow carbon nanowires and the large interlayer distance (0.37 nm) between the graphitic sheets, which agrees with the interlayered distance predicted by theor. calcns. to enable Na-ion insertion in carbon materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq9utH1EdajJbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liGuu0nGAl5YQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XotlShu78%253D&amp;md5=c54fcb809a340c023d9185f1d9f5e848</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit49" data-doi="10.1021/jp970490q"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lindstrom, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sodergren, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Solbrand, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Rensmo, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hjelm, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hagfeldt, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lindquist, S.-E.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Li<sup>+</sup> Ion Insertion in TiO<sub>2</sub> (Anatase). 2. Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">12</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">518</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">522</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/nmat3601</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D12%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D518-522%26journal%3DNat.%2BMater.%26author%3DV.%2BAugustyn%26author%3DJ.%2BCome%26author%3DM.%2BA.%2BLowe%26author%3DJ.%2BW.%2BKim%26author%3DP.%2BL.%2BTaberna%26author%3DS.%2BH.%2BTolbert%26author%3DH.%2BD.%2BAbruna%26author%3DP.%2BSimon%26author%3DB.%2BDunn%26title%3DHigh-Rate%2BElectrochemical%2BEnergy%2BStorage%2BThrough%2BLi%252B%2BIntercalation%2BPseudocapacitance%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fnmat3601&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.6b15887&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fnmat3601&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit50R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">50</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-rate electrochemical energy storage through Li+ intercalation pseudocapacitance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Augustyn, Veronica; Come, Jeremy; Lowe, Michael A.; Kim, Jong Woung; Taberna, Pierre-Louis; Tolbert, Sarah H.; Abruna, Hector D.; Simon, Patrice; Dunn, Bruce</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">518-522</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NMAACR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1476-1122</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Pseudocapacitance is commonly assocd. with surface or near-surface reversible redox reactions, as obsd. with RuO2·xH2O in an acidic electrolyte.  However, we recently demonstrated that a pseudocapacitive mechanism occurs when lithium ions are inserted into mesoporous and nanocrystal films of orthorhombic Nb2O5 (T-Nb2O5; refs , ).  Here, we quantify the kinetics of charge storage in T-Nb2O5: currents that vary inversely with time, charge-storage capacity that is mostly independent of rate, and redox peaks that exhibit small voltage offsets even at high rates.  We also define the structural characteristics necessary for this process, termed intercalation pseudocapacitance, which are a cryst. network that offers two-dimensional transport pathways and little structural change on intercalation.  The principal benefit realized from intercalation pseudocapacitance is that high levels of charge storage are achieved within short periods of time because there are no limitations from solid-state diffusion.  Thick electrodes (up to 40 μm thick) prepd. with T-Nb2O5 offer the promise of exploiting intercalation pseudocapacitance to obtain high-rate charge-storage devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrPatnkXnvsNbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liI1_8qpogdlw"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGonnrfiSUpaRrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh0pmXRn5b3kA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVOrtLjF&amp;md5=485b20393bb8876b2f69ca708aeba606</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1038/nmat2007"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ellis, B.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In the search for new pos.-electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries, recent research has focused on nanostructured lithium transition-metal phosphates that exhibit desirable properties such as high energy storage capacity combined with electrochem. stability.  Only one member of this class-the olivine LiFePO4 (ref. 3)-has risen to prominence so far, owing to its other characteristics, which include low cost, low environmental impact and safety.  These are crit. for large-capacity systems such as plug-in hybrid elec. vehicles.  Nonetheless, olivine has some inherent shortcomings, including 1-dimensional lithium-ion transport and a two-phase redox reaction that together limit the mobility of the phase boundary.  Thus, nanocrystallites are key to enable fast rate behavior.  Also probably the long-term economic viability of large-scale Li-ion energy storage systems could be ultimately limited by global lithium reserves, although this remains speculative at present.  Here, the authors report on a sodium/lithium iron phosphate, A2FePO4F (A = Na, Li), that could serve as a cathode in either Li-ion or Na-ion cells.  Also, it possesses facile two-dimensional pathways for Li+ transport, and the structural changes on redn.-oxidn. are minimal.  This results in a vol. change of only 3.7% that, unlike the olivine, contributes to the absence of distinct two-phase behavior during redox, and a reversible capacity that is 85% of theor.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoHTt31WeplQLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh0pmXRn5b3kA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2sXhtFWhtrjO&amp;md5=00fab3305d4ec64c11da74f07ad1ff76</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1039/c2ee02781j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Palomares, V.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Serras, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Villaluenga, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hueso, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh0pmXRn5b3kA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1039/c0ee00777c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zu, C. A thermodn. calcn. on the theor. energy densities of 1172 systems is performed and energy storage mechanisms are discussed, aiming to det. the theor. and practical limits of storing chem. energy and to screen possible systems.
